   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Unicode
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       For the 1889 Universal Telegraphic Phrase-book, see
       <a href="/wiki/Commercial_code_(communications)" title="Commercial code (communications)">
        Commercial code (communications)
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <table class="metadata mbox-small noprint selfref" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;width: 22em;">
       <tr>
        <td style="width:1px">
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;">
         <b>
          This article contains
          <a href="/wiki/Help:Special_characters" title="Help:Special characters">
           special characters
          </a>
          .
         </b>
         Without proper
         <a href="/wiki/Help:Special_characters" title="Help:Special characters">
          rendering support
         </a>
         , you may see
         <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">
          question marks, boxes, or other symbols
         </a>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Unicode_logo.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="108" data-file-width="108" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Unicode_logo.svg/180px-Unicode_logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Unicode_logo.svg/270px-Unicode_logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Unicode_logo.svg/360px-Unicode_logo.svg.png 2x" width="180"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Unicode_logo.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Logo of the
         <a href="/wiki/Unicode_Consortium" title="Unicode Consortium">
          Unicode Consortium
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <b>
        Unicode
       </b>
       is a computing industry standard for the consistent
       <a href="/wiki/Character_encoding" title="Character encoding">
        encoding
       </a>
       , representation, and handling of
       <a href="/wiki/Character_(computing)" title="Character (computing)">
        text
       </a>
       expressed in most of the world's
       <a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">
        writing systems
       </a>
       . The latest version contains a repertoire of 136,755
       <a href="/wiki/Character_(computing)" title="Character (computing)">
        characters
       </a>
       covering 139 modern and historic
       <a href="/wiki/Script_(Unicode)" title="Script (Unicode)">
        scripts
       </a>
       , as well as multiple symbol sets.
       <i>
        The Unicode Standard
       </i>
       is maintained in conjunction with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISO/IEC_10646" title="ISO/IEC 10646">
        ISO/IEC 10646
       </a>
       , and both are code-for-code identical.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        The Unicode Standard
       </i>
       consists of a set of code charts for visual reference, an encoding method and set of standard
       <a href="/wiki/Character_encoding" title="Character encoding">
        character encodings
       </a>
       , a set of reference
       <a href="/wiki/Data_file" title="Data file">
        data files
       </a>
       , and a number of related items, such as character properties, rules for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unicode_normalization" title="Unicode normalization">
        normalization
       </a>
       , decomposition,
       <a href="/wiki/Unicode_collation_algorithm" title="Unicode collation algorithm">
        collation
       </a>
       , rendering, and
       <a href="/wiki/Bi-directional_text" title="Bi-directional text">
        bidirectional
       </a>
       display order (for the correct display of text containing both right-to-left scripts, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">
        Arabic
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">
        Hebrew
       </a>
       , and left-to-right scripts).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As of June 2017
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , the most recent version is
       <i>
        Unicode 10.0
       </i>
       . The standard is maintained by the
       <a href="/wiki/Unicode_Consortium" title="Unicode Consortium">
        Unicode Consortium
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Unicode's success at unifying character sets has led to its widespread and predominant use in the
       <a href="/wiki/Internationalization_and_localization" title="Internationalization and localization">
        internationalization and localization
       </a>
       of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computer_software" title="Computer software">
        computer software
       </a>
       . The standard has been implemented in many recent technologies, including modern
       <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">
        operating systems
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/XML" title="XML">
        XML
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">
        Java
       </a>
       (and other programming languages), and the
       <a href="/wiki/.NET_Framework" title=".NET Framework">
        .NET Framework
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_Unicode_encodings" title="Comparison of Unicode encodings">
        Unicode can be implemented
       </a>
       by different
       <a href="/wiki/Character_encoding" title="Character encoding">
        character encodings
       </a>
       . The Unicode standard defines
       <a href="/wiki/UTF-8" title="UTF-8">
        UTF-8
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/UTF-16" title="UTF-16">
        UTF-16
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/UTF-32" title="UTF-32">
        UTF-32
       </a>
       , and several other encodings are in use. The most commonly used encodings are UTF-8, UTF-16 and
       <a href="/wiki/Universal_Coded_Character_Set" title="Universal Coded Character Set">
        UCS
       </a>
       -2, a precursor of UTF-16.
      </p>
      <p>
       UTF-8, dominantly used by websites (over 90%), uses one
       <a href="/wiki/Byte" title="Byte">
        byte
       </a>
       for the first 128 code points, and up to 4 bytes for other characters. The first 128 Unicode code points are the ASCII characters; so an ASCII text is a UTF-8 text.
      </p>
      <p>
       UCS-2 simply uses two bytes (16 bits) for each character but can only encode the first 65,536 code points, the so-called
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basic_Multilingual_Plane" title="Basic Multilingual Plane">
        Basic Multilingual Plane
       </a>
       (BMP). With 1,114,112 code points on 17 planes being possible, and with over 120,000 code points defined so far, many Unicode characters are beyond the reach of UCS-2. Therefore, UCS-2 is obsolete, though still widely used in software. UTF-16 extends UCS-2, by using the same
       <a href="/wiki/16-bit" title="16-bit">
        16-bit
       </a>
       encoding as UCS-2 for the Basic Multilingual Plane, and a 4-byte encoding for the other planes. As long as it contains no code points in the reserved range U+0D800-U+0DFFF, a UCS-2 text is a valid UTF-16 text.
      </p>
      <p>
       UTF-32 (also referred to as UCS-4) uses four bytes for each character. Like UCS-2, the number of bytes per character is fixed, facilitating character indexing; but unlike UCS-2, UTF-32 is able to encode all Unicode code points. However, because each character uses four bytes, UTF-32 takes significantly more space than other encodings, and is not widely used.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_and_development">
        Origin and development
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Origin and development">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Unicode has the explicit aim of transcending the limitations of traditional character encodings, such as those defined by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISO_8859" title="ISO 8859">
        ISO 8859
       </a>
       standard, which find wide usage in various countries of the world but remain largely incompatible with each other. Many traditional character encodings share a common problem in that they allow bilingual computer processing (usually using
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_character" title="Latin character">
        Latin characters
       </a>
       and the local script), but not multilingual computer processing (computer processing of arbitrary scripts mixed with each other).
      </p>
      <p>
       Unicode, in intent, encodes the underlying characters—
       <a href="/wiki/Grapheme" title="Grapheme">
        graphemes
       </a>
       and grapheme-like units—rather than the variant
       <a href="/wiki/Glyph" title="Glyph">
        glyphs
       </a>
       (renderings) for such characters. In the case of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_character" title="Chinese character">
        Chinese characters
       </a>
       , this sometimes leads to controversies over distinguishing the underlying character from its variant glyphs (see
       <a href="/wiki/Han_unification" title="Han unification">
        Han unification
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       In text processing, Unicode takes the role of providing a unique
       <i>
        code point
       </i>
       —a
       <a href="/wiki/Number" title="Number">
        number
       </a>
       , not a glyph—for each character. In other words, Unicode represents a character in an abstract way and leaves the visual rendering (size, shape,
       <a href="/wiki/Font" title="Font">
        font
       </a>
       , or style) to other software, such as a
       <a href="/wiki/Web_browser" title="Web browser">
        web browser
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Word_processor" title="Word processor">
        word processor
       </a>
       . This simple aim becomes complicated, however, because of concessions made by Unicode's designers in the hope of encouraging a more rapid adoption of Unicode.
      </p>
      <p>
       The first 256 code points were made identical to the content of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISO-8859-1" title="ISO-8859-1">
        ISO-8859-1
       </a>
       so as to make it trivial to convert existing western text. Many essentially identical characters were encoded multiple times at different code points to preserve distinctions used by legacy encodings and therefore, allow conversion from those encodings to Unicode (and back) without losing any information. For example, the "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fullwidth_form" title="Fullwidth form">
        fullwidth forms
       </a>
       " section of code points encompasses a full Latin alphabet that is separate from the main Latin alphabet section because in Chinese, Japanese, and Korean (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CJK" title="CJK">
        CJK
       </a>
       ) fonts, these Latin characters are rendered at the same width as CJK
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ideograph" title="Ideograph">
        ideographs
       </a>
       , rather than at half the width. For other examples, see
       <a href="/wiki/Duplicate_characters_in_Unicode" title="Duplicate characters in Unicode">
        duplicate characters in Unicode
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        <span id="Unicode_88">
        </span>
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Based on experiences with the
       <a href="/wiki/Xerox_Character_Code_Standard" title="Xerox Character Code Standard">
        Xerox Character Code Standard
       </a>
       (XCCS) since 1980,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unicode-88_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-unicode-88-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the origins of Unicode date to 1987, when
       <a href="/wiki/Joe_Becker_(Unicode)" title="Joe Becker (Unicode)">
        Joe Becker
       </a>
       from
       <a href="/wiki/Xerox" title="Xerox">
        Xerox
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lee_Collins_(software_engineer)" title="Lee Collins (software engineer)">
        Lee Collins
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mark_Davis_(Unicode)" title="Mark Davis (Unicode)">
        Mark Davis
       </a>
       from
       <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">
        Apple
       </a>
       started investigating the practicalities of creating a universal character set.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       With additional input from Peter Fenwick and Dave Opstad,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unicode-88_2-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-unicode-88-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Joe Becker published a draft proposal for an "international/multilingual text character encoding system in August 1988, tentatively called Unicode". He explained that "[t]he name 'Unicode' is intended to suggest a unique, unified, universal encoding".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unicode-88_2-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-unicode-88-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In this document, entitled
       <i>
        Unicode 88
       </i>
       , Becker outlined a
       <a href="/wiki/16-bit" title="16-bit">
        16-bit
       </a>
       character model:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unicode-88_2-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-unicode-88-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        Unicode is intended to address the need for a workable, reliable world text encoding. Unicode could be roughly described as "wide-body ASCII" that has been stretched to 16 bits to encompass the characters of all the world's living languages. In a properly engineered design, 16 bits per character are more than sufficient for this purpose.
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       His original 16-bit design was based on the assumption that only those scripts and characters in modern use would need to be encoded:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unicode-88_2-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-unicode-88-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        Unicode gives higher priority to ensuring utility for the future than to preserving past antiquities. Unicode aims in the first instance at the characters published in modern text (e.g. in the union of all newspapers and magazines printed in the world in 1988), whose number is undoubtedly far below 2
        <sup>
         14
        </sup>
        = 16,384. Beyond those modern-use characters, all others may be defined to be obsolete or rare; these are better candidates for private-use registration than for congesting the public list of generally useful Unicodes.
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       In early 1989, the Unicode working group expanded to include Ken Whistler and Mike Kernaghan of Metaphor, Karen Smith-Yoshimura and Joan Aliprand of
       <a href="/wiki/Research_Libraries_Group" title="Research Libraries Group">
        RLG
       </a>
       , and Glenn Wright of
       <a href="/wiki/Sun_Microsystems" title="Sun Microsystems">
        Sun Microsystems
       </a>
       , and in 1990, Michel Suignard and Asmus Freytag from
       <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">
        Microsoft
       </a>
       and Rick McGowan of
       <a href="/wiki/NeXT" title="NeXT">
        NeXT
       </a>
       joined the group. By the end of 1990, most of the work on mapping existing character encoding standards had been completed, and a final review draft of Unicode was ready.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Unicode_Consortium" title="Unicode Consortium">
        Unicode Consortium
       </a>
       was incorporated in California on January 3, 1991,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and in October 1991, the first volume of the Unicode standard was published. The second volume, covering Han ideographs, was published in June 1992.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1996, a surrogate character mechanism was implemented in Unicode 2.0, so that Unicode was no longer restricted to 16 bits. This increased the Unicode codespace to over a million code points, which allowed for the encoding of many historic scripts (e.g.,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egyptian_hieroglyph" title="Egyptian hieroglyph">
        Egyptian Hieroglyphs
       </a>
       ) and thousands of rarely used or obsolete characters that had not been anticipated as needing encoding. Among the characters not originally intended for Unicode are rarely used Kanji or Chinese characters, many of which are part of personal and place names, making them rarely used, but much more essential than envisioned in the original architecture of Unicode.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unicoderevisited_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-unicoderevisited-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Microsoft TrueType specification version 1.0 from 1992 used the name
       <i>
        Apple Unicode
       </i>
       instead of
       <i>
        Unicode
       </i>
       for the Platform ID in the naming table.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture_and_terminology">
        Architecture and terminology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Architecture and terminology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <span id="Upluslink">
       </span>
       Unicode defines a codespace of 1,114,112
       <a href="/wiki/Code_point" title="Code point">
        code points
       </a>
       in the range 0
       <sub>
        hex
       </sub>
       to 10FFFF
       <sub>
        hex
       </sub>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glossary_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Glossary-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Normally a Unicode code point is referred to by writing "U+" followed by its
       <a href="/wiki/Hexadecimal" title="Hexadecimal">
        hexadecimal
       </a>
       number. For code points in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basic_Multilingual_Plane" title="Basic Multilingual Plane">
        Basic Multilingual Plane
       </a>
       (BMP), four digits are used (e.g., U+0058 for the character LATIN CAPITAL LETTER X); for code points outside the BMP, five or six digits are used, as required (e.g., U+E0001 for the character LANGUAGE TAG and U+10FFFD for the character PRIVATE USE CHARACTER-10FFFD).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Code_point_planes_and_blocks">
        Code point planes and blocks
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Code point planes and blocks">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Plane_(Unicode)" title="Plane (Unicode)">
        Plane (Unicode)
       </a>
      </div>

       <i>

       </i>
       , numbered 0 to 16:
      </p>

          <br/>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Unicode/Character_reference/21000-21FFF" title="wikibooks:Unicode/Character reference/21000-21FFF">
           21000–​21FFF
          </a>
          <br/>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Unicode/Character_reference/22000-22FFF" title="wikibooks:Unicode/Character reference/22000-22FFF">
           22000–​22FFF
          </a>
          <br/>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Unicode/Character_reference/23000-23FFF" title="wikibooks:Unicode/Character reference/23000-23FFF">
           23000–​23FFF
          </a>
          <br/>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Unicode/Character_reference/24000-24FFF" title="wikibooks:Unicode/Character reference/24000-24FFF">
           24000–​24FFF
          </a>
          <br/>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Unicode/Character_reference/25000-25FFF" title="wikibooks:Unicode/Character reference/25000-25FFF">
           25000–​25FFF
          </a>
          <br/>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Unicode/Character_reference/26000-26FFF" title="wikibooks:Unicode/Character reference/26000-26FFF">
           26000–​26FFF
          </a>
          <br/>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Unicode/Character_reference/27000-27FFF" title="wikibooks:Unicode/Character reference/27000-27FFF">
           27000–​27FFF
          </a>
         </p>
        </td>
        <td style="background:#efefef;">
         <p>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Unicode/Character_reference/28000-28FFF" title="wikibooks:Unicode/Character reference/28000-28FFF">
           28000–​28FFF
          </a>
          <br/>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Unicode/Character_reference/29000-29FFF" title="wikibooks:Unicode/Character reference/29000-29FFF">
           29000–​29FFF
          </a>
          <br/>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Unicode/Character_reference/2A000-2AFFF" title="wikibooks:Unicode/Character reference/2A000-2AFFF">
           2A000–​2AFFF
          </a>
          <br/>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Unicode/Character_reference/2B000-2BFFF" title="wikibooks:Unicode/Character reference/2B000-2BFFF">
           2B000–​2BFFF
          </a>
          <br/>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Unicode/Character_reference/2C000-2CFFF" title="wikibooks:Unicode/Character reference/2C000-2CFFF">
           2C000–​2CFFF
          </a>
          <br/>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Unicode/Character_reference/2D000-2DFFF" title="wikibooks:Unicode/Character reference/2D000-2DFFF">
           2D000–​2DFFF
          </a>
          <br/>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Unicode/Character_reference/2E000-2EFFF" title="wikibooks:Unicode/Character reference/2E000-2EFFF">
           2E000–​2EFFF
          </a>
          <br/>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Unicode/Character_reference/2F000-2FFFF" title="wikibooks:Unicode/Character reference/2F000-2FFFF">
           2F000–​2FFFF
          </a>
         </p>
        </td>
        <td style="background:#f7f7f7;">
        </td>
        <td style="background:#efefef;">
         <p>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Unicode/Character_reference/E0000-E0FFF" title="wikibooks:Unicode/Character reference/E0000-E0FFF">
           E0000–​E0FFF
          </a>
         </p>
        </td>
        <td style="background:#f7f7f7; padding:0px;">
         <p>
          <i>
           15: SPUA-A
          </i>
          <br/>
          F0000–​FFFFF
          <br/>
          <br/>
          <i>
           16: SPUA-B
          </i>
          <br/>
          100000–​10FFFF
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       All code points in the BMP are accessed as a single code unit in
       <a href="/wiki/UTF-16" title="UTF-16">
        UTF-16
       </a>
       encoding and can be encoded in one, two or three bytes in
       <a href="/wiki/UTF-8" title="UTF-8">
        UTF-8
       </a>
       . Code points in Planes 1 through 16 (
       <i>
        supplementary planes
       </i>
       ) are accessed as surrogate pairs in UTF-16 and encoded in four bytes in UTF-8.
      </p>
      <p>
       Within each plane, characters are allocated within named
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Block_(Unicode)" title="Block (Unicode)">
         blocks
        </a>
       </i>
       of related characters. Although blocks are an arbitrary size, they are always a multiple of 16 code points and often a multiple of 128 code points. Characters required for a given script may be spread out over several different blocks.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="General_Category_property">
        General Category property
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: General Category property">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Each code point has a single
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Character_property_(Unicode)#General_Category" title="Character property (Unicode)">
        General Category
       </a>
       property. The major categories are denoted: Letter, Mark, Number, Punctuation, Symbol, Separator and Other. Within these categories, there are subdivisions. The General Category is not useful for every use, since legacy encodings have used multiple characteristics per single code point. E.g.,
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
         U+000A
        </span>
       </span>
       <span style="font-size:small">
        &lt;control-000A&gt;
       </span>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Line_feed" title="Line feed">
        Line feed
       </a>
       (LF) in ASCII is both a control and a formatting separator; in Unicode the General Category is "Other, Control". Often, other properties must be used to specify the characteristics and behaviour of a code point. The possible General Categories are:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable sortable collapsible collapsed" style="width:100%; margin:0;">
       <tr>

       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td data-sort-value="063">

        </td>

        </td>

        </td>

        </td>

        </td>

         <span class="nowrap">

          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basic_Multilingual_Plane" title="Basic Multilingual Plane">
           BMP
          </a>
         </span>
         , 131,068
         <span class="nowrap">

          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Private_Use_Area" title="Private Use Area">

       <tr class="sortbottom">
        <td colspan="6">
         <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">

              </cite>
              <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Unicode+Standard&amp;rft.atitle=Table+2-3%3A+Types+of+code+points&amp;rft.date=2017-07&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.unicode.org%2Fversions%2FUnicode10.0.0%2Fch02.pdf%23G286941&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AUnicode">

             <span class="reference-text">

              </a>
              Stability policy: Some gc groups will never change. gc=Nd corresponds with Numeric Type=De (decimal).
             </span>
            </li>
            <li id="cite_note-No_name_use_label-11">
             <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
              ^
              <a href="#cite_ref-No_name_use_label_11-0">
               <sup>
                <i>
                 <b>

               </sup>

              <a href="#cite_ref-No_name_use_label_11-2">
               <sup>

                  c
                 </b>
                </i>
               </sup>
              </a>
              <a href="#cite_ref-No_name_use_label_11-3">
               <sup>
                <i>
                 <b>
                  d
                 </b>
                </i>
               </sup>
              </a>
              <a href="#cite_ref-No_name_use_label_11-4">
               <sup>
                <i>
                 <b>
                  e
                 </b>
                </i>
               </sup>
              </a>
             </span>
             <span class="reference-text">
              <cite class="citation web">
               <a class="external text" href="http://www.unicode.org/versions/Unicode10.0.0/ch04.pdf#G135875" rel="nofollow">
                "Table 4-13: Construction of Code Point Labels"
               </a>
               <span style="font-size:85%;">
                (PDF)
               </span>
               .
               <i>
                The Unicode Standard
               </i>
               . Unicode Consortium. July 2017.
              </cite>
              <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Unicode+Standard&amp;rft.atitle=Table+4-13%3A+Construction+of+Code+Point+Labels&amp;rft.date=2017-07&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.unicode.org%2Fversions%2FUnicode10.0.0%2Fch04.pdf%23G135875&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AUnicode">
               <span style="display:none;">
               </span>
              </span>
              A
              <i>
               Code Point Label
              </i>
              may be used to identify a nameless code point. E.g. &lt;control-
              <i>
               hhhh
              </i>
              &gt;, &lt;control-0088&gt;. The Name remains blank, which can prevent inadvertently replacing, in documentation, a Control Name with a true Control code. Unicode also uses &lt;not a character&gt; for &lt;noncharacter&gt;.
             </span>
            </li>
           </ol>
          </div>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Code points in the range U+D800–U+DBFF (1,024 code points) are known as high-surrogate code points, and code points in the range U+DC00–U+DFFF (1,024 code points) are known as low-surrogate code points. A high-surrogate code point (also known as a leading surrogate) followed by a low-surrogate code point (also known as a trailing surrogate) together form a surrogate pair used in
       <a href="/wiki/UTF-16" title="UTF-16">
        UTF-16
       </a>
       to represent 1,048,576 code points outside BMP. High and low surrogate code points are not valid by themselves. Thus the range of code points that are available for use as characters is U+0000–U+D7FF and U+E000–U+10FFFF (1,112,064 code points). The value of these code points (i.e., excluding surrogates) is sometimes referred to as the character's scalar value.
      </p>
      <p>
       Certain non-character code points are guaranteed never to be used for encoding characters, although applications may make use of these code points internally if they wish. There are sixty-six noncharacters: U+FDD0–U+FDEF and any code point ending in the value FFFE or FFFF (i.e., U+FFFE, U+FFFF, U+1FFFE, U+1FFFF, … U+10FFFE, U+10FFFF). The set of noncharacters is stable, and no new noncharacters will ever be defined.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stability-policy_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-stability-policy-16">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Reserved code points are those code points which are available for use as encoded characters, but are not yet defined as characters by Unicode.
      </p>
      <p>
       Private-use code points are considered to be assigned characters, but they have no interpretation specified by the Unicode standard
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       so any interchange of such characters requires an agreement between sender and receiver on their interpretation. There are three private-use areas in the Unicode codespace:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Private Use Area: U+E000–U+F8FF (6,400 characters)
       </li>
       <li>
        Supplementary Private Use Area-A: U+F0000–U+FFFFD (65,534 characters)
       </li>
       <li>
        Supplementary Private Use Area-B: U+100000–U+10FFFD (65,534 characters).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Graphic characters are characters defined by Unicode to have a particular semantic, and either have a visible
       <a href="/wiki/Glyph" title="Glyph">
        glyph
       </a>
       shape or represent a visible space. As of Unicode 10.0 there are 136,537 graphic characters.
      </p>
      <p>
       Format characters are characters that do not have a visible appearance, but may have an effect on the appearance or behavior of neighboring characters. For example,
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
         U+200C
        </span>
       </span>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zero_width_non-joiner" title="Zero width non-joiner">
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
         Zero width non-joiner
        </span>
       </a>
       and
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
         U+200D
        </span>
       </span>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zero_width_joiner" title="Zero width joiner">
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
         Zero width joiner
        </span>
       </a>
       may be used to change the default shaping behavior of adjacent characters (e.g., to inhibit ligatures or request ligature formation). There are 153 format characters in Unicode 10.0.
      </p>
      <p>
       Sixty-five code points (U+0000–U+001F and U+007F–U+009F) are reserved as control codes, and correspond to the C0 and C1 control codes defined in ISO/IEC 6429. Of these U+0009 (Tab), U+000A (Line Feed), and U+000D (Carriage Return) are widely used in Unicode-encoded texts.
      </p>
      <p>
       Graphic characters, format characters, control code characters, and private use characters are known collectively as
       <i>
        assigned characters
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Abstract_characters">
        Abstract characters
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Abstract characters">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The set of graphic and format characters defined by Unicode does not correspond directly to the repertoire of
       <i>
        abstract characters
       </i>
       that is representable under Unicode. Unicode encodes characters by associating an abstract character with a particular code point.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, not all abstract characters are encoded as a single Unicode character, and some abstract characters may be represented in Unicode by a sequence of two or more characters. For example, a Latin small letter "i" with an
       <a href="/wiki/Ogonek" title="Ogonek">
        ogonek
       </a>
       , a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dot_above" title="Dot above">
        dot above
       </a>
       , and an
       <a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">
        acute accent
       </a>
       , which is required in
       <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">
        Lithuanian
       </a>
       , is represented by the character sequence U+012F, U+0307, U+0301. Unicode maintains a list of uniquely named character sequences for abstract characters that are not directly encoded in Unicode.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       All graphic, format, and private use characters have a unique and immutable name by which they may be identified. This immutability has been guaranteed since Unicode version 2.0 by the Name Stability policy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stability-policy_16-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-stability-policy-16">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In cases where the name is seriously defective and misleading, or has a serious typographical error, a formal alias may be defined, and applications are encouraged to use the formal alias in place of the official character name. For example,
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
         U+A015
        </span>
       </span>
       <span style="font-size:125%">
        ꀕ
       </span>
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
        YI SYLLABLE WU
       </span>
       has the formal alias
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        yi syllable iteration mark
       </span>
       , and
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
         U+FE18
        </span>
       </span>
       <span style="font-size:125%">
        ︘
       </span>
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
        PRESENTATION FORM FOR VERTICAL RIGHT WHITE LENTICULAR BRA
        <b>
         KC
        </b>
        ET
       </span>
       (sic) has the formal alias
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        presentation form for vertical right white lenticular bracket
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Unicode_Consortium">
        Unicode Consortium
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Unicode Consortium">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Unicode_Consortium" title="Unicode Consortium">
        Unicode Consortium
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Unicode Consortium is a nonprofit organization that coordinates Unicode's development. Full members include most of the main computer software and hardware companies with any interest in text-processing standards, including
       <a href="/wiki/Adobe_Systems" title="Adobe Systems">
        Adobe Systems
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">
        Apple
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">
        Google
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Business_Machines" title="International Business Machines">
        IBM
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">
        Microsoft
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Oracle_Corporation" title="Oracle Corporation">
        Oracle Corporation
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!" title="Yahoo!">
        Yahoo!
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Consortium has the ambitious goal of eventually replacing existing character encoding schemes with Unicode and its standard
       <a href="#Unicode_Transformation_Format_and_Universal_Character_Set">
        Unicode Transformation Format
       </a>
       (UTF) schemes, as many of the existing schemes are limited in size and scope and are incompatible with
       <a href="/wiki/Multilingualism" title="Multilingualism">
        multilingual
       </a>
       environments.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Versions">
        <span id="1.0.0">
        </span>
        <span id="1.0.1">
        </span>
        <span id="1.1">
        </span>
        <span id="2.0">
        </span>
        <span id="2.1">
        </span>
        <span id="3.0">
        </span>
        <span id="3.1">
        </span>
        <span id="3.2">
        </span>
        <span id="4.0">
        </span>
        <span id="4.1">
        </span>
        <span id="5.0">
        </span>
        <span id="5.1">
        </span>
        <span id="5.2">
        </span>
        <span id="6.0">
        </span>
        <span id="6.1">
        </span>
        <span id="6.2">
        </span>
        <span id="6.3">
        </span>
        <span id="7.0">
        </span>
        <span id="8.0">
        </span>
        <span id="9.0">
        </span>
        <span id="10.0">
        </span>
        <span id="11.0">
        </span>
        <span id="12.0">
        </span>
        Versions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Versions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Unicode is developed in conjunction with the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">
        International Organization for Standardization
       </a>
       and shares the character repertoire with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISO/IEC_10646" title="ISO/IEC 10646">
        ISO/IEC 10646
       </a>
       : the Universal Character Set. Unicode and ISO/IEC 10646 function equivalently as character encodings, but
       <i>
        The Unicode Standard
       </i>
       contains much more information for implementers, covering—in depth—topics such as bitwise encoding,
       <a href="/wiki/Unicode_collation_algorithm" title="Unicode collation algorithm">
        collation
       </a>
       and rendering. The Unicode Standard enumerates a multitude of character properties, including those needed for supporting
       <a href="/wiki/Bi-directional_text" title="Bi-directional text">
        bidirectional text
       </a>
       . The two standards do use slightly different terminology.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Consortium first published
       <i>
        The Unicode Standard
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
        ISBN
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-321-18578-1" title="Special:BookSources/0-321-18578-1">
        0-321-18578-1
       </a>
       ) in 1991 and continues to develop standards based on that original work. The latest version of the standard, Unicode 10.0, was released in June 2017 and is available from the consortium's website. The last of the major versions (versions x.0) to be published in book form was Unicode 5.0 (
       <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
        ISBN
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-321-48091-0" title="Special:BookSources/0-321-48091-0">
        0-321-48091-0
       </a>
       ), but since Unicode 6.0 the full text of the standard is no longer being published in book form. In 2012, however, it was announced that only the core specification for Unicode version 6.1 would be made available as a 692-page print-on-demand paperback.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-version6.1PoD_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-version6.1PoD-22">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Unlike the previous major version printings of the Standard, the print-on-demand core specification does not include any code charts or standard annexes, but the entire standard, including the core specification, will still remain freely available on the Unicode website.
      </p>
      <p>
       Thus far, the following major and minor versions of the Unicode standard have been published. Update versions, which do not include any changes to character repertoire, are signified by the third number (e.g., "version 4.0.1") and are omitted in the table below.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <caption>
        Unicode versions
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <th rowspan="2">
         Version
        </th>
        <th rowspan="2">
         Date
        </th>
        <th rowspan="2">
         Book
        </th>
        <th rowspan="2">
         Corresponding
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_Character_Set" title="Universal Character Set">
          ISO/IEC 10646
         </a>
         edition
        </th>
        <th rowspan="2">
         <a href="/wiki/Script_(Unicode)" title="Script (Unicode)">
          Scripts
         </a>
        </th>
        <th colspan="2">
         Characters
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Total
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
          <a href="#cite_note-24">
           [tablenote 1]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </th>
        <th>
         Notable additions
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>

         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Mandaic_alphabet" title="Mandaic alphabet">
          Mandaic
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Playing_card" title="Playing card">
          playing card
         </a>
         symbols,
         <a href="/wiki/Traffic_sign" title="Traffic sign">
          transport
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Map" title="Map">
          map
         </a>
         symbols,
         <a href="/wiki/Alchemical_symbol" title="Alchemical symbol">
          alchemical symbols
         </a>
         ,
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emoticons" title="Emoticons">
          emoticons
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Emoji" title="Emoji">
          emoji
         </a>
         . 222 additional
         <a href="/wiki/CJK_Unified_Ideographs" title="CJK Unified Ideographs">
          CJK Unified Ideographs
         </a>
         (CJK-D) added.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
          <a href="#cite_note-38">
           [33]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>

      <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
       <div class="mw-references-wrap">

            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">

          </span>
         </li>
        </ol>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Scripts_covered">
        Scripts covered
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Scripts covered">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Script_(Unicode)" title="Script (Unicode)">
        Script (Unicode)
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Unicode_sample.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="162" data-file-width="582" height="56" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Unicode_sample.png/200px-Unicode_sample.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Unicode_sample.png/300px-Unicode_sample.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Unicode_sample.png/400px-Unicode_sample.png 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Unicode_sample.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Many modern applications can render a substantial subset of the many
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scripts_in_Unicode" title="Scripts in Unicode">
          scripts in Unicode
         </a>
         , as demonstrated by this screenshot from the
         <a href="/wiki/OpenOffice.org" title="OpenOffice.org">
          OpenOffice.org
         </a>
         application.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Unicode covers almost all scripts (
       <a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">
        writing systems
       </a>
       ) in current use today.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
          <span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (October 2013)">
           not in citation given
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A total of 139
       <a href="/wiki/Script_(Unicode)" title="Script (Unicode)">
        scripts
       </a>
       are included in the latest version of Unicode (covering
       <a href="/wiki/Alphabet" title="Alphabet">
        alphabets
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Abugida" title="Abugida">
        abugidas
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Syllabary" title="Syllabary">
        syllabaries
       </a>
       ), although there are still scripts that are not yet encoded, particularly those mainly used in historical, liturgical, and academic contexts. Further additions of characters to the already encoded scripts, as well as symbols, in particular for mathematics and
       <a href="/wiki/Musical_notation" title="Musical notation">
        music
       </a>
       (in the form of notes and rhythmic symbols), also occur.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Unicode Roadmap Committee (
       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Everson" title="Michael Everson">
        Michael Everson
       </a>
       , Rick McGowan, and Ken Whistler) maintain the list of scripts that are candidates or potential candidates for encoding and their tentative code block assignments on the
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.unicode.org/roadmaps/" rel="nofollow">
        Unicode Roadmap
       </a>
       page of the
       <a href="/wiki/Unicode_Consortium" title="Unicode Consortium">
        Unicode Consortium
       </a>
       Web site. For some scripts on the Roadmap, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Jurchen_script" title="Jurchen script">
        Jurchen
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Khitan_small_script" title="Khitan small script">
        Khitan small script
       </a>
       , encoding proposals have been made and they are working their way through the approval process. For others scripts, such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maya_hieroglyphics" title="Maya hieroglyphics">
        Mayan
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Rongorongo" title="Rongorongo">
        Rongorongo
       </a>
       , no proposal has yet been made, and they await agreement on character repertoire and other details from the user communities involved.
      </p>
      <p>
       Some modern invented scripts which have not yet been included in Unicode (e.g.,
       <a href="/wiki/Tengwar" title="Tengwar">
        Tengwar
       </a>
       ) or which do not qualify for inclusion in Unicode due to lack of real-world use (e.g.,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Klingon_writing_systems" title="Klingon writing systems">
        Klingon
       </a>
       ) are listed in the
       <a href="/wiki/ConScript_Unicode_Registry" title="ConScript Unicode Registry">
        ConScript Unicode Registry
       </a>
       , along with unofficial but widely used
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Private_Use_(Unicode)" title="Private Use (Unicode)">
        Private Use Area
       </a>
       code assignments.
      </p>
      <p>
       There is also a
       <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Unicode_Font_Initiative" title="Medieval Unicode Font Initiative">
        Medieval Unicode Font Initiative
       </a>
       focused on special Latin medieval characters. Part of these proposals have been already included into Unicode.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="external text" href="http://linguistics.berkeley.edu/sei/" rel="nofollow">
        Script Encoding Initiative
       </a>
       , a project run by Deborah Anderson at the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">
        University of California, Berkeley
       </a>
       was founded in 2002 with the goal of funding proposals for scripts not yet encoded in the standard. The project has become a major source of proposed additions to the standard in recent years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mapping_and_encodings">
        Mapping and encodings
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Mapping and encodings">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Universal_Character_Set_characters" title="Universal Character Set characters">
        Universal Character Set characters
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Several mechanisms have been specified for implementing Unicode. The choice depends on available storage space,
       <a href="/wiki/Source_code" title="Source code">
        source code
       </a>
       compatibility, and interoperability with other systems.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Unicode_Transformation_Format_and_Universal_Coded_Character_Set">
        <span id="UTF">
        </span>
        <span id="UCS">
        </span>
        Unicode Transformation Format and Universal Coded Character Set
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Unicode Transformation Format and Universal Coded Character Set">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Unicode defines two mapping methods: the
       <i>
        Unicode Transformation Format
       </i>
       (UTF) encodings, and the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Universal_Coded_Character_Set" title="Universal Coded Character Set">
         Universal Coded Character Set
        </a>
       </i>
       (UCS) encodings. An encoding maps (possibly a subset of) the range of Unicode
       <i>
        code points
       </i>
       to sequences of values in some fixed-size range, termed
       <i>
        code values
       </i>
       . All UTF encodings map all code points (except surrogates) to a unique sequence of bytes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The numbers in the names of the encodings indicate the number of bits per code value (for UTF encodings) or the number of bytes per code value (for UCS encodings). UTF-8 and UTF-16 are probably the most commonly used encodings. UCS-2 is an obsolete subset of UTF-16; UCS-4 and UTF-32 are functionally equivalent.
      </p>
      <p>
       UTF encodings include:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/UTF-1" title="UTF-1">
         UTF-1
        </a>
        , a retired predecessor of UTF-8, maximizes compatibility with
        <a href="/wiki/ISO/IEC_2022" title="ISO/IEC 2022">
         ISO 2022
        </a>
        , no longer part of
        <i>
         The Unicode Standard
        </i>
        ;
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/UTF-7" title="UTF-7">
         UTF-7
        </a>
        , a 7-bit encoding sometimes used in e-mail, often considered obsolete (not part of
        <i>
         The Unicode Standard
        </i>
        , but only documented as an informational
        <a href="/wiki/Request_for_Comments" title="Request for Comments">
         RFC
        </a>
        , i.e., not on the Internet Standards Track either);
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/UTF-8" title="UTF-8">
         UTF-8
        </a>
        , an
        <a href="/wiki/8-bit" title="8-bit">
         8-bit
        </a>
        variable-width encoding which maximizes compatibility with
        <a href="/wiki/ASCII" title="ASCII">
         ASCII
        </a>
        ;
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/UTF-EBCDIC" title="UTF-EBCDIC">
         UTF-EBCDIC
        </a>
        , an 8-bit variable-width encoding similar to UTF-8, but designed for compatibility with
        <a href="/wiki/EBCDIC" title="EBCDIC">
         EBCDIC
        </a>
        (not part of
        <i>
         The Unicode Standard
        </i>
        );
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/UTF-16" title="UTF-16">
         UTF-16
        </a>
        , a 16-bit, variable-width encoding;
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/UTF-32" title="UTF-32">
         UTF-32
        </a>
        , a
        <a href="/wiki/32-bit" title="32-bit">
         32-bit
        </a>
        , fixed-width encoding.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       UTF-8 uses one to four bytes per code point and, being compact for Latin scripts and ASCII-compatible, provides the
       <i>
        de facto
       </i>
       standard encoding for interchange of Unicode text. It is used by
       <a href="/wiki/FreeBSD" title="FreeBSD">
        FreeBSD
       </a>
       and most recent
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linux_distributions" title="Linux distributions">
        Linux distributions
       </a>
       as a direct replacement for legacy encodings in general text handling.
      </p>
      <p>
       The UCS-2 and UTF-16 encodings specify the Unicode
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byte_Order_Mark" title="Byte Order Mark">
        Byte Order Mark
       </a>
       (BOM) for use at the beginnings of text files, which may be used for byte ordering detection (or
       <a href="/wiki/Endianness" title="Endianness">
        byte endianness
       </a>
       detection). The BOM, code point U+FEFF has the important property of unambiguity on byte reorder, regardless of the Unicode encoding used; U+FFFE (the result of byte-swapping U+FEFF) does not equate to a legal character, and U+FEFF in other places, other than the beginning of text, conveys the zero-width non-break space (a character with no appearance and no effect other than preventing the formation of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ligature_(typography)" title="Ligature (typography)">
        ligatures
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       The same character converted to UTF-8 becomes the byte sequence
       <code>
        EF BB BF
       </code>
       . The Unicode Standard allows that the BOM "can serve as signature for UTF-8 encoded text where the character set is unmarked".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some software developers have adopted it for other encodings, including UTF-8, in an attempt to distinguish UTF-8 from local 8-bit
       <a href="/wiki/Code_page" title="Code page">
        code pages
       </a>
       . However
       <a class="external mw-magiclink-rfc" href="https://tools.ietf.org/html/rfc3629" rel="nofollow">
        RFC 3629
       </a>
       , the UTF-8 standard, recommends that byte order marks be forbidden in protocols using UTF-8, but discusses the cases where this may not be possible. In addition, the large restriction on possible patterns in UTF-8 (for instance there cannot be any lone bytes with the high bit set) means that it should be possible to distinguish UTF-8 from other character encodings without relying on the BOM.
      </p>
      <p>
       In UTF-32 and UCS-4, one 32-bit code value serves as a fairly direct representation of any character's code point (although the endianness, which varies across different platforms, affects how the code value manifests as an octet sequence). In the other encodings, each code point may be represented by a variable number of code values. UTF-32 is widely used as an internal representation of text in programs (as opposed to stored or transmitted text), since every Unix operating system that uses the
       <a href="/wiki/GNU_Compiler_Collection" title="GNU Compiler Collection">
        gcc
       </a>
       compilers to generate software uses it as the standard "
       <a href="/wiki/Wide_character" title="Wide character">
        wide character
       </a>
       " encoding. Some programming languages, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Seed7" title="Seed7">
        Seed7
       </a>
       , use UTF-32 as internal representation for strings and characters. Recent versions of the
       <a href="/wiki/Python_(programming_language)" title="Python (programming language)">
        Python
       </a>
       programming language (beginning with 2.2) may also be configured to use UTF-32 as the representation for Unicode strings, effectively disseminating such encoding in
       <a href="/wiki/High-level_programming_language" title="High-level programming language">
        high-level
       </a>
       coded software.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Punycode" title="Punycode">
        Punycode
       </a>
       , another encoding form, enables the encoding of Unicode strings into the limited character set supported by the
       <a href="/wiki/ASCII" title="ASCII">
        ASCII
       </a>
       -based
       <a href="/wiki/Domain_Name_System" title="Domain Name System">
        Domain Name System
       </a>
       (DNS). The encoding is used as part of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IDNA" title="IDNA">
        IDNA
       </a>
       , which is a system enabling the use of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internationalized_Domain_Names" title="Internationalized Domain Names">
        Internationalized Domain Names
       </a>
       in all scripts that are supported by Unicode. Earlier and now historical proposals include
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTF-5" title="UTF-5">
        UTF-5
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTF-6" title="UTF-6">
        UTF-6
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GB18030" title="GB18030">
        GB18030
       </a>
       is another encoding form for Unicode, from the
       <a href="/wiki/Standardization_Administration_of_China" title="Standardization Administration of China">
        Standardization Administration of China
       </a>
       . It is the official
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Character_set" title="Character set">
        character set
       </a>
       of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">
        People's Republic of China
       </a>
       (PRC).
       <a href="/wiki/Binary_Ordered_Compression_for_Unicode" title="Binary Ordered Compression for Unicode">
        BOCU-1
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_Compression_Scheme_for_Unicode" title="Standard Compression Scheme for Unicode">
        SCSU
       </a>
       are Unicode compression schemes. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/April_Fools%27_Day_RFC" title="April Fools' Day RFC">
        April Fools' Day RFC
       </a>
       of 2005 specified two
       <a href="/wiki/Parody" title="Parody">
        parody
       </a>
       UTF encodings,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTF-9" title="UTF-9">
        UTF-9
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTF-18" title="UTF-18">
        UTF-18
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ready-made_versus_composite_characters">
        Ready-made versus composite characters
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ready-made versus composite characters">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Unicode includes a mechanism for modifying character shape that greatly extends the supported glyph repertoire. This covers the use of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combining_diacritical_mark" title="Combining diacritical mark">
        combining diacritical marks
       </a>
       . They are inserted after the main character. Multiple combining diacritics may be stacked over the same character. Unicode also contains
       <a href="/wiki/Precomposed_character" title="Precomposed character">
        precomposed
       </a>
       versions of most letter/diacritic combinations in normal use. These make conversion to and from legacy encodings simpler, and allow applications to use Unicode as an internal text format without having to implement combining characters. For example,
       <i>
        é
       </i>
       can be represented in Unicode as
       <a href="#Upluslink">
        U+
       </a>
       0065 (LATIN SMALL LETTER E) followed by U+0301 (COMBINING ACUTE ACCENT), but it can also be represented as the precomposed character U+00E9 (LATIN SMALL LETTER E WITH ACUTE). Thus, in many cases, users have multiple ways of encoding the same character. To deal with this, Unicode provides the mechanism of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canonical_equivalence" title="Canonical equivalence">
        canonical equivalence
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       An example of this arises with
       <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">
        Hangul
       </a>
       , the Korean alphabet. Unicode provides a mechanism for composing Hangul syllables with their individual subcomponents, known as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hangul_Jamo" title="Hangul Jamo">
        Hangul Jamo
       </a>
       . However, it also provides 11,172 combinations of precomposed syllables made from the most common jamo.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CJK" title="CJK">
        CJK
       </a>
       ideographs currently have codes only for their precomposed form. Still, most of those ideographs comprise simpler elements (often called radicals in English), so in principle, Unicode could have decomposed them, as it did with Hangul. This would have greatly reduced the number of required code points, while allowing the display of virtually every conceivable ideograph (which might do away with some of the problems caused by
       <a href="/wiki/Han_unification" title="Han unification">
        Han unification
       </a>
       ). A similar idea is used by some
       <a href="/wiki/Input_method" title="Input method">
        input methods
       </a>
       , such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cangjie_method" title="Cangjie method">
        Cangjie
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Wubi_method" title="Wubi method">
        Wubi
       </a>
       . However, attempts to do this for character encoding have stumbled over the fact that ideographs do not decompose as simply or as regularly as Hangul does.
      </p>
      <p>
       A set of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radical_(Chinese_character)" title="Radical (Chinese character)">
        radicals
       </a>
       was provided in Unicode 3.0 (CJK radicals between U+2E80 and U+2EFF, KangXi radicals in U+2F00 to U+2FDF, and ideographic description characters from U+2FF0 to U+2FFB), but the Unicode standard (ch. 12.2 of Unicode 5.2) warns against using
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ideographic_Description_Sequences" title="Ideographic Description Sequences">
        ideographic description sequences
       </a>
       as an alternate representation for previously encoded characters:
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        This process is different from a formal
        <i>
         encoding
        </i>
        of an ideograph. There is no canonical description of unencoded ideographs; there is no semantic assigned to described ideographs; there is no equivalence defined for described ideographs. Conceptually, ideographic descriptions are more akin to the English phrase "an 'e' with an acute accent on it" than to the character sequence &lt;U+0065, U+0301&gt;.
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ligatures">
        Ligatures
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Ligatures">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Many scripts, including
       <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">
        Arabic
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Devan%C4%81gar%C4%AB" title="Devanāgarī">
        Devanagari
       </a>
       , have special orthographic rules that require certain combinations of letterforms to be combined into special
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ligature_(typography)" title="Ligature (typography)">
        ligature forms
       </a>
       . The rules governing ligature formation can be quite complex, requiring special script-shaping technologies such as ACE (Arabic Calligraphic Engine by DecoType in the 1980s and used to generate all the Arabic examples in the printed editions of the Unicode Standard), which became the
       <a href="/wiki/Proof_of_concept" title="Proof of concept">
        proof of concept
       </a>
       for
       <a href="/wiki/OpenType" title="OpenType">
        OpenType
       </a>
       (by Adobe and Microsoft),
       <a href="/wiki/Graphite_(SIL)" title="Graphite (SIL)">
        Graphite
       </a>
       (by
       <a href="/wiki/SIL_International" title="SIL International">
        SIL International
       </a>
       ), or
       <a href="/wiki/Apple_Advanced_Typography" title="Apple Advanced Typography">
        AAT
       </a>
       (by Apple).
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Font_language&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Font language (page does not exist)">
        Instructions
       </a>
       are also embedded in fonts to tell the
       <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">
        operating system
       </a>
       how to properly output different character sequences. A simple solution to the placement of combining marks or diacritics is assigning the marks a width of zero and placing the glyph itself to the left or right of the left
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sidebearing&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sidebearing (page does not exist)">
        sidebearing
       </a>
       (depending on the direction of the script they are intended to be used with). A mark handled this way will appear over whatever character precedes it, but will not adjust its position relative to the width or height of the base glyph; it may be visually awkward and it may overlap some glyphs. Real stacking is impossible, but can be approximated in limited cases (for example, Thai top-combining vowels and tone marks can just be at different heights to start with). Generally this approach is only effective in monospaced fonts, but may be used as a fallback rendering method when more complex methods fail.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Standardized_subsets">
        Standardized subsets
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Standardized subsets">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Several subsets of Unicode are standardized: Microsoft Windows since
       <a href="/wiki/Windows_NT_4.0" title="Windows NT 4.0">
        Windows NT 4.0
       </a>
       supports
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WGL-4" title="WGL-4">
        WGL-4
       </a>
       with 652 characters, which is considered to support all contemporary European languages using the Latin, Greek, or Cyrillic script. Other standardized subsets of Unicode include the Multilingual European Subsets:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       MES-1 (Latin scripts only, 335 characters), MES-2 (Latin, Greek and Cyrillic 1062 characters)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and MES-3A &amp; MES-3B (two larger subsets, not shown here). Note that MES-2 includes every character in MES-1 and WGL-4.
      </p>
