         <a href="/wiki/Block_Elements" title="Block Elements">
          Block Elements
         </a>
         (80–9F)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <b>
          A0–A1, AA–AC, B2, BA, BC, C4, CA–CB, CF, D8–D9, E6
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Geometric_Shapes" title="Geometric Shapes">
          Geometric Shapes
         </a>
         (A0–FF)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         26
        </th>
        <td>
         <b>
          3A–3C, 40, 42, 60, 63, 65–66,
          <i>
           6A,
          </i>
          6B
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Miscellaneous_Symbols" title="Miscellaneous Symbols">
          Miscellaneous Symbols
         </a>
         (00–FF)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         F0
        </th>
        <td>
         (01–02)
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Private_Use_Area_(Unicode_block)" title="Private Use Area (Unicode block)">
          Private Use Area
         </a>
         (00–FF ...)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         FB
        </th>
        <td>
         <b>
          01–02
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Alphabetic_Presentation_Forms" title="Alphabetic Presentation Forms">
          Alphabetic Presentation Forms
         </a>
         (00–4F)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         FF
        </th>
        <td>
         FD
        </td>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)" title="Specials (Unicode block)">
          Specials
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Rendering software which cannot process a Unicode character appropriately often displays it as an open rectangle, or the Unicode "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Replacement_character" title="Replacement character">
        replacement character
       </a>
       " (U+FFFD, �), to indicate the position of the unrecognized character. Some systems have made attempts to provide more information about such characters. Apple's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_Resort_font" title="Last Resort font">
        Last Resort font
       </a>
       will display a substitute glyph indicating the Unicode range of the character, and the
       <a href="/wiki/SIL_International" title="SIL International">
        SIL International
       </a>
       's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unicode_fallback_font" title="Unicode fallback font">
        Unicode Fallback
       </a>
       font will display a box showing the hexadecimal scalar value of the character.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Adoption">
        Adoption
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Adoption">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Operating_systems">
        Operating systems
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Operating systems">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Unicode has become the dominant scheme for internal processing and storage of text. Although a great deal of text is still stored in legacy encodings, Unicode is used almost exclusively for building new information processing systems. Early adopters tended to use
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UCS-2" title="UCS-2">
        UCS-2
       </a>
       (the fixed-width two-byte precursor to UTF-16) and later moved to
       <a href="/wiki/UTF-16" title="UTF-16">
        UTF-16
       </a>
       (the variable-width current standard), as this was the least disruptive way to add support for non-BMP characters. The best known such system is
       <a href="/wiki/Windows_NT" title="Windows NT">
        Windows NT
       </a>
       (and its descendants,
       <a href="/wiki/Windows_2000" title="Windows 2000">
        Windows 2000
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Windows_XP" title="Windows XP">
        Windows XP
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Windows_Vista" title="Windows Vista">
        Windows Vista
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Windows_7" title="Windows 7">
        Windows 7
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Windows_8" title="Windows 8">
        Windows 8
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Windows_10" title="Windows 10">
        Windows 10
       </a>
       ), which uses UTF-16 as the sole internal character encoding. The
       <a href="/wiki/Java_virtual_machine" title="Java virtual machine">
        Java
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/.NET_Framework" title=".NET Framework">
        .NET
       </a>
       bytecode environments,
       <a href="/wiki/MacOS" title="MacOS">
        macOS
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/KDE" title="KDE">
        KDE
       </a>
       also use it for internal representation. Unicode is available on
       <a href="/wiki/Windows_95" title="Windows 95">
        Windows 95
       </a>
       through
       <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Layer_for_Unicode" title="Microsoft Layer for Unicode">
        Microsoft Layer for Unicode
       </a>
       , as well as on its descendants,
       <a href="/wiki/Windows_98" title="Windows 98">
        Windows 98
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Windows_ME" title="Windows ME">
        Windows ME
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/UTF-8" title="UTF-8">
        UTF-8
       </a>
       (originally developed for
       <a href="/wiki/Plan_9_from_Bell_Labs" title="Plan 9 from Bell Labs">
        Plan 9
       </a>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       has become the main storage encoding on most
       <a href="/wiki/Unix-like" title="Unix-like">
        Unix-like
       </a>
       operating systems (though others are also used by some libraries) because it is a relatively easy replacement for traditional
       <a href="/wiki/Extended_ASCII" title="Extended ASCII">
        extended ASCII
       </a>
       character sets. UTF-8 is also the most common Unicode encoding used in
       <a href="/wiki/HTML" title="HTML">
        HTML
       </a>
       documents on the
       <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web" title="World Wide Web">
        World Wide Web
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Multilingual text-rendering engines which use Unicode include
       <a href="/wiki/Uniscribe" title="Uniscribe">
        Uniscribe
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/DirectWrite" title="DirectWrite">
        DirectWrite
       </a>
       for Microsoft Windows,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ATSUI" title="ATSUI">
        ATSUI
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Core_Text" title="Core Text">
        Core Text
       </a>
       for macOS, and
       <a href="/wiki/Pango" title="Pango">
        Pango
       </a>
       for
       <a href="/wiki/GTK%2B" title="GTK+">
        GTK+
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/GNOME" title="GNOME">
        GNOME
       </a>
       desktop.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Input_methods">
        Input methods
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Input methods">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Unicode_input" title="Unicode input">
        Unicode input
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Because keyboard layouts cannot have simple key combinations for all characters, several operating systems provide alternative input methods that allow access to the entire repertoire.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/ISO/IEC_14755" title="ISO/IEC 14755">
        ISO/IEC 14755
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which standardises methods for entering Unicode characters from their code points, specifies several methods. There is the
       <i>
        Basic method
       </i>
       , where a
       <i>
        beginning sequence
       </i>
       is followed by the hexadecimal representation of the code point and the
       <i>
        ending sequence
       </i>
       . There is also a
       <i>
        screen-selection entry method
       </i>
       specified, where the characters are listed in a table in a screen, such as with a character map program.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Email">
        Email
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Email">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Unicode_and_email" title="Unicode and email">
        Unicode and email
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/MIME" title="MIME">
        MIME
       </a>
       defines two different mechanisms for encoding non-ASCII characters in
       <a href="/wiki/Email" title="Email">
        email
       </a>
       , depending on whether the characters are in email headers (such as the "Subject:"), or in the text body of the message; in both cases, the original character set is identified as well as a transfer encoding. For email transmission of Unicode, the
       <a href="/wiki/UTF-8" title="UTF-8">
        UTF-8
       </a>
       character set and the
       <a href="/wiki/Base64" title="Base64">
        Base64
       </a>
       or the
       <a href="/wiki/Quoted-printable" title="Quoted-printable">
        Quoted-printable
       </a>
       transfer encoding are recommended, depending on whether much of the message consists of
       <a href="/wiki/ASCII" title="ASCII">
        ASCII
       </a>
       characters. The details of the two different mechanisms are specified in the MIME standards and generally are hidden from users of email software.
      </p>
      <p>
       The adoption of Unicode in email has been very slow. Some East Asian text is still encoded in encodings such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISO-2022" title="ISO-2022">
        ISO-2022
       </a>
       , and some devices, such as mobile phones, still cannot correctly handle Unicode data. Support has been improving, however. Many major free mail providers such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yahoo" title="Yahoo">
        Yahoo
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">
        Google
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Gmail" title="Gmail">
        Gmail
       </a>
       ), and
       <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">
        Microsoft
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Outlook.com" title="Outlook.com">
        Outlook.com
       </a>
       ) support it.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Web">
        Web
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Web">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Unicode_and_HTML" title="Unicode and HTML">
        Unicode and HTML
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       All
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W3C" title="W3C">
        W3C
       </a>
       recommendations have used Unicode as their
       <i>
        document character set
       </i>
       since HTML 4.0.
       <a href="/wiki/Web_browser" title="Web browser">
        Web browsers
       </a>
       have supported Unicode, especially UTF-8, for many years. There used to be display problems resulting primarily from
       <a href="/wiki/Typeface" title="Typeface">
        font
       </a>
       related issues; e.g. v 6 and older of Microsoft
       <a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer" title="Internet Explorer">
        Internet Explorer
       </a>
       did not render many code points unless explicitly told to use a font that contains them.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Although syntax rules may affect the order in which characters are allowed to appear,
       <a href="/wiki/XML" title="XML">
        XML
       </a>
       (including
       <a href="/wiki/XHTML" title="XHTML">
        XHTML
       </a>
       ) documents, by definition,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       comprise characters from most of the Unicode code points, with the exception of:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        most of the
        <a href="/wiki/C0_and_C1_control_codes" title="C0 and C1 control codes">
         C0 control codes
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        the permanently unassigned code points D800–DFFF
       </li>
       <li>
        FFFE or FFFF
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       HTML characters manifest either directly as
       <a href="/wiki/Byte" title="Byte">
        bytes
       </a>
       according to document's encoding, if the encoding supports them, or users may write them as numeric character references based on the character's Unicode code point. For example, the references
       <code>
        &amp;#916;
       </code>
       ,
       <code>
        &amp;#1049;
       </code>
       ,
       <code>
        &amp;#1511;
       </code>
       ,
       <code>
        &amp;#1605;
       </code>
       ,
       <code>
        &amp;#3671;
       </code>
       ,
       <code>
        &amp;#12354;
       </code>
       ,
       <code>
        &amp;#21494;
       </code>
       ,
       <code>
        &amp;#33865;
       </code>
       , and
       <code>
        &amp;#47568;
       </code>
       (or the same numeric values expressed in hexadecimal, with
       <code>
        &amp;#x
       </code>
       as the prefix) should display on all browsers as Δ, Й, ק ,م, ๗, あ, 叶, 葉, and 말.
      </p>
      <p>
       When specifying
       <a href="/wiki/Uniform_Resource_Identifier" title="Uniform Resource Identifier">
        URIs
       </a>
       , for example as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uniform_Resource_Locator" title="Uniform Resource Locator">
        URLs
       </a>
       in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HTTP" title="HTTP">
        HTTP
       </a>
       requests, non-ASCII characters must be
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Percent_encoding" title="Percent encoding">
        percent-encoded
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Fonts">
        Fonts
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Fonts">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unicode_typeface" title="Unicode typeface">
        Unicode typeface
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Free and retail
       <a href="/wiki/Font" title="Font">
        fonts
       </a>
       based on Unicode are widely available, since
       <a href="/wiki/TrueType" title="TrueType">
        TrueType
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/OpenType" title="OpenType">
        OpenType
       </a>
       support Unicode. These font formats map Unicode code points to glyphs.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_typefaces" title="List of typefaces">
        Thousands of fonts
       </a>
       exist on the market, but fewer than a dozen fonts—sometimes described as "pan-Unicode" fonts—attempt to support the majority of Unicode's character repertoire. Instead, Unicode-based
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Unicode_fonts" title="List of Unicode fonts">
        fonts
       </a>
       typically focus on supporting only basic ASCII and particular scripts or sets of characters or symbols. Several reasons justify this approach: applications and documents rarely need to render characters from more than one or two writing systems; fonts tend to demand resources in computing environments; and operating systems and applications show increasing intelligence in regard to obtaining glyph information from separate font files as needed, i.e.,
       <a href="/wiki/Font_substitution" title="Font substitution">
        font substitution
       </a>
       . Furthermore, designing a consistent set of rendering instructions for tens of thousands of glyphs constitutes a monumental task; such a venture passes the point of
       <a href="/wiki/Diminishing_returns" title="Diminishing returns">
        diminishing returns
       </a>
       for most typefaces.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Newlines">
        Newlines
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Newlines">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Unicode partially addresses the
       <a href="/wiki/Newline" title="Newline">
        newline
       </a>
       problem that occurs when trying to read a text file on different platforms. Unicode defines a large number of
       <a href="/wiki/Newline#Unicode" title="Newline">
        characters
       </a>
       that conforming applications should recognize as line terminators.
      </p>
      <p>
       In terms of the newline, Unicode introduced
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
         U+2028
        </span>
       </span>
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
        LINE SEPARATOR
       </span>
       and
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
         U+2029
        </span>
       </span>
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
        PARAGRAPH SEPARATOR
       </span>
       . This was an attempt to provide a Unicode solution to encoding paragraphs and lines semantically, potentially replacing all of the various platform solutions. In doing so, Unicode does provide a way around the historical platform dependent solutions. Nonetheless, few if any Unicode solutions have adopted these Unicode line and paragraph separators as the sole canonical line ending characters. However, a common approach to solving this issue is through newline normalization. This is achieved with the Cocoa text system in Mac OS X and also with W3C XML and HTML recommendations. In this approach every possible newline character is converted internally to a common newline (which one does not really matter since it is an internal operation just for rendering). In other words, the text system can correctly treat the character as a newline, regardless of the input's actual encoding.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Issues">
        Issues
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Issues">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophical_and_completeness_criticisms">
        Philosophical and completeness criticisms
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Philosophical and completeness criticisms">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Han_unification" title="Han unification">
        Han unification
       </a>
       (the identification of forms in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Asian_language" title="East Asian language">
        East Asian languages
       </a>
       which one can treat as stylistic variations of the same historical character) has become one of the most controversial aspects of Unicode, despite the presence of a majority of experts from all three regions in the
       <a href="/wiki/Ideographic_Rapporteur_Group" title="Ideographic Rapporteur Group">
        Ideographic Rapporteur Group
       </a>
       (IRG), which advises the Consortium and ISO on additions to the repertoire and on Han unification.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Unicode has been criticized for failing to separately encode older and alternative forms of
       <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">
        kanji
       </a>
       which, critics argue, complicates the processing of ancient Japanese and uncommon Japanese names. This is often due to the fact that Unicode encodes characters rather than glyphs (the visual representations of the basic character that often vary from one language to another). Unification of glyphs leads to the perception that the languages themselves, not just the basic character representation, are being merged.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dw2001_61-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-dw2001-61">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
          <span title='"and, contains" and meaning of statement (April 2010)'>
           clarification needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       There have been several attempts to create alternative encodings that preserve the stylistic differences between Chinese, Japanese, and Korean characters in opposition to Unicode's policy of Han unification. An example of one is
       <a href="/wiki/TRON_(encoding)" title="TRON (encoding)">
        TRON
       </a>
       (although it is not widely adopted in Japan, there are some users who need to handle historical Japanese text and favor it).
      </p>
      <p>
       Although the repertoire of fewer than 21,000 Han characters in the earliest version of Unicode was largely limited to characters in common modern usage, Unicode now includes more than 87,000 Han characters, and work is continuing to add thousands more historic and dialectal characters used in China, Japan, Korea, Taiwan, and Vietnam.
      </p>
      <p>
       Modern font technology provides a means to address the practical issue of needing to depict a unified Han character in terms of a collection of alternative glyph representations, in the form of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Variation_Selectors" title="Variation Selectors">
        Unicode variation sequences
       </a>
       . For example, the Advanced Typographic tables of
       <a href="/wiki/OpenType" title="OpenType">
        OpenType
       </a>
       permit one of a number of alternative glyph representations to be selected when performing the character to glyph mapping process. In this case, information can be provided within plain text to designate which alternate character form to select.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Special_Cyrillics.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="395" data-file-width="261" height="333" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Special_Cyrillics.png/220px-Special_Cyrillics.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c0/Special_Cyrillics.png 1.5x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Special_Cyrillics.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Various
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyrillic" title="Cyrillic">
          Cyrillic
         </a>
         characters shown with and without italics.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       If the difference in the appropriate glyphs for two characters in the same script differ only in the italic, Unicode has generally unified them, as can be seen in the comparison between Russian (labeled standard) and Serbian characters at right, meaning that the differences are displayed through smart font technology or manually changing fonts.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mapping_to_legacy_character_sets">
        Mapping to legacy character sets
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Mapping to legacy character sets">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Unicode was designed to provide code-point-by-code-point
       <a href="/wiki/Round-trip_format_conversion" title="Round-trip format conversion">
        round-trip format conversion
       </a>
       to and from any preexisting character encodings, so that text files in older character sets can be converted to Unicode and then back and get back the same file, without employing context-dependent interpretation. That has meant that inconsistent legacy architectures, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Combining_character" title="Combining character">
        combining diacritics
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Precomposed_character" title="Precomposed character">
        precomposed characters
       </a>
       , both exist in Unicode, giving more than one method of representing some text. This is most pronounced in the three different encoding forms for Korean
       <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">
        Hangul
       </a>
       . Since version 3.0, any precomposed characters that can be represented by a combining sequence of already existing characters can no longer be added to the standard in order to preserve interoperability between software using different versions of Unicode.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Injective" title="Injective">
        Injective
       </a>
       mappings must be provided between characters in existing legacy character sets and characters in Unicode to facilitate conversion to Unicode and allow interoperability with legacy software. Lack of consistency in various mappings between earlier Japanese encodings such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shift-JIS" title="Shift-JIS">
        Shift-JIS
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EUC-JP" title="EUC-JP">
        EUC-JP
       </a>
       and Unicode led to
       <a href="/wiki/Round-trip_format_conversion" title="Round-trip format conversion">
        round-trip format conversion
       </a>
       mismatches, particularly the mapping of the character JIS X 0208 '～' (1-33, WAVE DASH), heavily used in legacy database data, to either
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
         U+FF5E
        </span>
       </span>
       <span style="font-size:125%">
        ～
       </span>
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
        FULLWIDTH TILDE
       </span>
       (in
       <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">
        Microsoft Windows
       </a>
       ) or
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
         U+301C
        </span>
       </span>
       <span style="font-size:125%">
        〜
       </span>
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
        WAVE DASH
       </span>
       (other vendors).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Some Japanese computer programmers objected to Unicode because it requires them to separate the use of
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
         U+005C
        </span>
       </span>
       <span style="font-size:125%">
        \
       </span>
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
        REVERSE SOLIDUS
       </span>
       (backslash) and
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
         U+00A5
        </span>
       </span>
       <span style="font-size:125%">
        ¥
       </span>
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
        YEN SIGN
       </span>
       , which was mapped to 0x5C in JIS X 0201, and a lot of legacy code exists with this usage.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (This encoding also replaces tilde '~' 0x7E with macron '¯', now 0xAF.) The separation of these characters exists in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISO_8859-1" title="ISO 8859-1">
        ISO 8859-1
       </a>
       , from long before Unicode.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Indic_scripts">
        Indic scripts
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Indic scripts">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indic_script" title="Indic script">
        Indic scripts
       </a>
       such as
       <a href="/wiki/Tamil_script" title="Tamil script">
        Tamil
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Devanagari" title="Devanagari">
        Devanagari
       </a>
       are each allocated only 128 code points, matching the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISCII" title="ISCII">
        ISCII
       </a>
       standard. The correct rendering of Unicode Indic text requires transforming the stored logical order characters into visual order and the forming of ligatures (aka conjuncts) out of components. Some local scholars argued in favor of assignments of Unicode code points to these ligatures, going against the practice for other writing systems, though Unicode contains some Arabic and other ligatures for backward compatibility purposes only.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Encoding of any new ligatures in Unicode will not happen, in part because the set of ligatures is font-dependent, and Unicode is an encoding independent of font variations. The same kind of issue arose for the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tibetan_script" title="Tibetan script">
        Tibetan script
       </a>
       in 2003 when the
       <a href="/wiki/Standardization_Administration_of_China" title="Standardization Administration of China">
        Standardization Administration of China
       </a>
       proposed encoding 956 precomposed Tibetan syllables,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but these were rejected for encoding by the relevant ISO committee (
       <a href="/wiki/ISO/IEC_JTC_1/SC_2" title="ISO/IEC JTC 1/SC 2">
        ISO/IEC JTC 1/SC 2
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Thai_alphabet" title="Thai alphabet">
        Thai alphabet
       </a>
       support has been criticized for its ordering of Thai characters. The vowels เ, แ, โ, ใ, ไ that are written to the left of the preceding consonant are in visual order instead of phonetic order, unlike the Unicode representations of other Indic scripts. This complication is due to Unicode inheriting the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TIS-620" title="TIS-620">
        Thai Industrial Standard 620
       </a>
       , which worked in the same way, and was the way in which Thai had always been written on keyboards. This ordering problem complicates the Unicode collation process slightly, requiring table lookups to reorder Thai characters for collation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dw2001_61-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-dw2001-61">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Even if Unicode had adopted encoding according to spoken order, it would still be problematic to collate words in dictionary order. E.g., the word
       <span lang="es" xml:lang="es">
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E0%B9%81%E0%B8%AA%E0%B8%94%E0%B8%87#Thai" title="wikt:แสดง">
         แสดง
        </a>
       </span>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Thai_and_Lao" title="Help:IPA/Thai and Lao">
         [sa dɛːŋ]
        </a>
       </span>
       "perform" starts with a consonant cluster "สด" (with an inherent vowel for the consonant "ส"), the vowel แ-, in spoken order would come after the ด, but in a dictionary, the word is collated as it is written, with the vowel following the ส.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Combining_characters">
        Combining characters
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Combining characters">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Combining_character" title="Combining character">
        Combining character
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unicode_normalization#Normalization" title="Unicode normalization">
        Unicode normalization § Normalization
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Characters with diacritical marks can generally be represented either as a single precomposed character or as a decomposed sequence of a base letter plus one or more non-spacing marks. For example, ḗ (precomposed e with macron and acute above) and ḗ (e followed by the combining macron above and combining acute above) should be rendered identically, both appearing as an
       <a href="/wiki/E" title="E">
        e
       </a>
       with a
       <a href="/wiki/Macron_(diacritic)" title="Macron (diacritic)">
        macron
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">
        acute accent
       </a>
       , but in practice, their appearance may vary depending upon what rendering engine and fonts are being used to display the characters. Similarly,
       <a href="/wiki/Dot_(diacritic)" title="Dot (diacritic)">
        underdots
       </a>
       , as needed in the
       <a href="/wiki/Romanization" title="Romanization">
        romanization
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Indo-Aryan languages">
        Indic
       </a>
       , will often be placed incorrectly
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2011)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       . Unicode characters that map to precomposed glyphs can be used in many cases, thus avoiding the problem, but where no precomposed character has been encoded the problem can often be solved by using a specialist Unicode font such as
       <a href="/wiki/Charis_SIL" title="Charis SIL">
        Charis SIL
       </a>
       that uses
       <a href="/wiki/Graphite_(SIL)" title="Graphite (SIL)">
        Graphite
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/OpenType" title="OpenType">
        OpenType
       </a>
       , or
       <a href="/wiki/Apple_Advanced_Typography" title="Apple Advanced Typography">
        AAT
       </a>
       technologies for advanced rendering features.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Anomalies">
        Anomalies
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Anomalies">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Unicode standard has imposed rules intended to guarantee stability.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Depending on the strictness of a rule, a change can be prohibited or allowed. For example, a "name" given to a code point can not and will not change. But a "script" property is more flexible, by Unicode's own rules. In version 2.0, Unicode changed many code point "names" from version 1. At the same moment, Unicode stated that from then on, an assigned name to a code point will never change anymore. This implies that when mistakes are published, these mistakes cannot be corrected, even if they are trivial (as happened in one instance with the spelling BRAKCET for BRACKET in a character name). In 2006 a list of anomalies in character names was first published, for example:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tn17_70-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-tn17-70">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <span class="nowrap">
         <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
          U+2118
         </span>
        </span>
        <span style="font-size:125%">
         ℘
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/Weierstrass_p" title="Weierstrass p">
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
          script capital p
         </span>
        </a>
        (HTML
        <code>
         &amp;#8472;
        </code>
        <b>
         ·
        </b>
        <code>
         &amp;weierp;
        </code>
        ): it is not a capital
        <dl>
         <dd>
          The name says "capital", but it is a small letter. The true capital is
          <span class="nowrap">
           <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
            U+1D4AB
           </span>
          </span>
          <span style="font-size:125%">
           𝒫
          </span>
          <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
           MATHEMATICAL SCRIPT CAPITAL P
          </span>
          (HTML
          <code>
           &amp;#119979;
          </code>
          )
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
           <a href="#cite_note-71">
            [66]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="nowrap">
         <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
          U+034F
         </span>
        </span>
        <span style="font-size:125%">
         ͏
        </span>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combining_grapheme_joiner" title="Combining grapheme joiner">
         <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
          COMBINING GRAPHEME JOINER
         </span>
        </a>
        (HTML
        <code>
         &amp;#847;
        </code>
        ): Does not join graphemes.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tn17_70-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-tn17-70">
          [65]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="nowrap">
         <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
          U+A015
         </span>
        </span>
        <span style="font-size:125%">
         ꀕ
        </span>
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
         YI SYLLABLE WU
        </span>
        (HTML
        <code>
         &amp;#40981;
        </code>
        ): This is not a Yi syllable, but a Yi iteration mark. Its name, however, cannot be changed due to the policy of the Consortium.
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="nowrap">
         <span class="monospaced" style="font-family: monospace, monospace;">
          U+FE18
         </span>
        </span>
        <span style="font-size:125%">
         ︘
        </span>
        <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-size: smaller;">
         PRESENTATION FORM FOR VERTICAL RIGHT WHITE LENTICULAR BRAKCET
        </span>
        (HTML
        <code>
         &amp;#65048;
        </code>
        ):
        <i>
         bracket
        </i>
        is spelled incorrectly. Since this is the fixed character name by policy, it cannot be changed.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
         <a href="#cite_note-72">
          [67]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="References">
        References
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Unicode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: References">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
       <ol class="references">
        <li id="cite_note-1">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-1">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation web">
           <a class="external text" href="http://www.unicode.org/standard/principles.html" rel="nofollow">
            "The Unicode Standard: A Technical Introduction"
           </a>
           <span class="reference-accessdate">
            . Retrieved
            <span class="nowrap">
             2010-03-16
            </span>
           </span>
           .
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=The+Unicode+Standard%3A+A+Technical+Introduction&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.unicode.org%2Fstandard%2Fprinciples.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AUnicode">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-unicode-88-2">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          ^
          <a href="#cite_ref-unicode-88_2-0">
           <sup>
            <i>
             <b>
              a
             </b>
            </i>
           </sup>
          </a>
          <a href="#cite_ref-unicode-88_2-1">
           <sup>
            <i>
             <b>
              b
             </b>
            </i>
           </sup>
          </a>
          <a href="#cite_ref-unicode-88_2-2">
           <sup>
            <i>
             <b>
              c
             </b>
            </i>
           </sup>
          </a>
          <a href="#cite_ref-unicode-88_2-3">
           <sup>
            <i>
             <b>
              d
             </b>
            </i>
           </sup>
          </a>
          <a href="#cite_ref-unicode-88_2-4">
           <sup>
            <i>
             <b>
              e
             </b>
            </i>
           </sup>
          </a>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation web">
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_D._Becker" title="Joseph D. Becker">
            Becker, Joseph D.
           </a>
           (1998-09-10) [1988-08-29].
           <a class="external text" href="http://unicode.org/history/unicode88.pdf" rel="nofollow">
            "Unicode 88"
           </a>
           <span style="font-size:85%;">
            (PDF)
           </span>
           .
           <i>
            unicode.org
           </i>
           (10th anniversary reprint ed.).
           <a href="/wiki/Unicode_Consortium" title="Unicode Consortium">
            Unicode Consortium
           </a>
           .
           <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20161125224409/http://unicode.org/history/unicode88.pdf" rel="nofollow">
            Archived
           </a>
           <span style="font-size:85%;">
            (PDF)
           </span>
           from the original on 2016-11-25
           <span class="reference-accessdate">
            . Retrieved
            <span class="nowrap">
             2016-10-25
            </span>
           </span>
           .
           <q>
            In 1978, the initial proposal for a set of "Universal Signs" was made by
            <a href="/wiki/Bob_Belleville" title="Bob Belleville">
             Bob Belleville
            </a>
            at
            <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xerox_PARC" title="Xerox PARC">
             Xerox PARC
            </a>
            . Many persons contributed ideas to the development of a new encoding design. Beginning in 1980, these efforts evolved into the
            <a href="/wiki/Xerox_Character_Code_Standard" title="Xerox Character Code Standard">
             Xerox Character Code Standard
            </a>
            (XCCS) by the present author, a multilingual encoding which has been maintained by Xerox as an internal corporate standard since 1982, through the efforts of Ed Smura, Ron Pellar, and others.
            <br/>
            Unicode arose as the result of eight years of working experience with XCCS. Its fundamental differences from XCCS were proposed by Peter Fenwick and Dave Opstad (pure 16-bit codes), and by
            <a href="/wiki/Lee_Collins_(Unicode)" title="Lee Collins (Unicode)">
             Lee Collins
            </a>
            (ideographic character unification). Unicode retains the many features of XCCS whose utility have been proved over the years in an international line of communication multilingual system products.
           </q>
          </cite>

         </a>
         , (
         <a class="external autonumber" href="http://www.unicode.org/versions/Unicode6.0.0/" rel="nofollow">
          [1]
         </a>
         ).
        </li>
        <li>
         <i>
          The Complete Manual of Typography
         </i>
         , James Felici, Adobe Press; 1st edition, 2002.
         <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
          ISBN
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-321-12730-7" title="Special:BookSources/0-321-12730-7">
          0-321-12730-7
         </a>
        </li>

         <i>
          Unicode: A Primer
         </i>
         , Tony Graham, M&amp;T books, 2000.
         <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
          ISBN
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-7645-4625-2" title="Special:BookSources/0-7645-4625-2">
          0-7645-4625-2
         </a>
         .
        </li>
        <li>
         <i>

         <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">

         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-201-70052-2" title="Special:BookSources/0-201-70052-2">
